Intensely complicated math yields a digital lock that cannot be picked in any reasonable period of time. If you want to see the communication, you either get the password, or you give up.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EDigital encryption is at work right now, as you read this: Yahoo Tech (and all Yahoo sites) use encryption that scrambles the data flowing between it and your browser. And your Web-mail service is increasingly likely to \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ftech\u002Fexplained-how-tls-keeps-your-email-secure-88310223169.html\"\u003Euse encryption\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, too, so your messages can&#x2019;t be read if they are intercepted.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EHow iOS&#xA0;8 or Android Lollipop differ from most encryption is where the\u003Cem\u003E key\u003C\u002Fem\u003E goes. Instead of appearing on multiple servers, it&#x2019;s a snowflake of a secret, unique and isolated to one device. It&#x2019;s generated and kept on the device; even the manufacturers cannot get the key to decrypt a phone.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EComey and other law enforcement veterans want Apple and Google to relax the robustness. As former U.S. Attorney General Michael Mukasey \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.politico.com\u002Fmorningcybersecurity\u002F1014\u002Fmorningcybersecurity15685.html\"\u003Eput it an event in Washington last month\u003C\u002Fa\u003E: &#x201C;The toothpaste needs to get back in the tube.&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWhat if it isn&#x2019;t put back, and we&#x2019;re in a world where unbreakable crypto is widely available and used?\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EWe don&#x2019;t need to speculate, because that world has existed since the 1990s. Pretty Good Privacy &#x2014; an open-source crypto program the government tried to quash by \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fnews.cnet.com\u002FFeds-drop-charges-in-encryption-case\u002F2100-1023_3-201991.html\"\u003Einvestigating developer Phil Zimmermann\u003C\u002Fa\u003E for violating weapons-export laws &#x2014; also doesn&#x2019;t offer any \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.washingtonpost.com\u002Fopinions\u002Fcompromise-needed-on-smartphone-encryption\u002F2014\u002F10\u002F03\u002F96680bf8-4a77-11e4-891d-713f052086a0_story.html\"\u003E&#x201C;golden key&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fa\u003E for the police.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EPGP is not the easiest app to set up, but Apple&#x2019;s \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fsupport.apple.com\u002Fen-us\u002FHT4790\"\u003EFileVault\u003C\u002Fa\u003E has been in OS&#xA0;X since 2011, while \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwindows.microsoft.com\u002Fen-us\u002Fwindows7\u002Fproducts\u002Ffeatures\u002Fbitlocker\"\u003EMicrosoft&#x2019;s BitLocker\u003C\u002Fa\u003E has been in some Windows releases for even longer. And there&#x2019;s no evidence of either \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.schneier.com\u002Fblog\u002Farchives\u002F2012\u002F08\u002Fan_analysis_of.html\"\u003EApple\u003C\u002Fa\u003E or \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fmashable.com\u002F2013\u002F09\u002F11\u002Ffbi-microsoft-bitlocker-backdoor\u002F\"\u003EMicrosoft\u003C\u002Fa\u003E having built in back doors that police officers or other agencies can use.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EResorting to other tricks\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003EYet police investigations still catch the bad guys. At one extreme, investigators have used court orders to \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.cnet.com\u002Fnews\u002Ffeds-use-keylogger-to-thwart-pgp-hushmail\u002F\"\u003Eplant malware on suspects&#x2019; machines to record passwords\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. At the other, there&#x2019;s traditional police work: \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ffirstlook.org\u002Ftheintercept\u002F\"\u003EThe Intercept&#x2019;s\u003C\u002Fa\u003E Dan Froomkin and Natasha Vargas-Cooper noted that \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Ffirstlook.org\u002Ftheintercept\u002F2014\u002F10\u002F17\u002Fdraft-two-cases-cited-fbi-dude-dumb-dumb\u002F\"\u003Eencryption had nothing to do with solving three of the four cases Comey cited in his speech\u003C\u002Fa\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cspan\u003EFor the law-abiding among us, encryption is a good thing &#x2014; as the FBI notes in \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.fbi.gov\u002Fabout-us\u002Finvestigate\u002Fcounterintelligence\u002Fbusiness-travel-brochure\"\u003Eits advice to business travelers\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003Cspan\u003E. \u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003Cspan\u003EBut even if you never leave the United States and have no corporate foes, knowing that your data can&#x2019;t be sucked out of your phone or computer should give you comfort.\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003EAnd knowing that there&#x2019;s some back door or master key that can be handed over to a governmental agency, or stolen by a criminal one, should make that comfort vanish. It is a rare vulnerability that stays locked up in one lab; just ask a former National Security Agency contractor who learned much about the agency&#x2019;s surveillance during his time there.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cp\u003E&#x201C;These things get found all the time, and they make a product completely untrustworthy and unreliable,&#x201D; Edward Snowden said via a remote and encrypted video interview at \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fwww.rcfp.org\u002Fencryptnews\"\u003Ea digital-security workshop for journalists\u003C\u002Fa\u003E&#xA0;on Nov.&#xA0;7. &#x201C;You&#x2019;ll see a whole bunch of intelligence agencies exploiting the same capability.&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n\u003Cdiv\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003E\u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fjohnjay.jjay.cuny.edu\u002Fprofiles\u002Fpublic_management.aspx?key=%5Bemail%5D=%27awandt@jjay.cuny.edu%27\"\u003EAdam Wandt\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, a professor and deputy chair at the John Jay College of Criminal Justice in New York, said the encryption on iOS&#xA0;8 and Android&#xA0;5.0 is harder to crack than past encryption systems &#x2014; in part because of requests from police and courts for better security on their own devices. But could you revise them to hide a secret backup key that only police and courts can access? For now, he said, &#x201C;it&#x2019;s near impossible.&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003EMukasey&#x2019;s description of the Internet at last month&#x2019;s event also applies to any crypto back door: &#x201C;Like a gun, it doesn&#x2019;t know whether to defend or attack.&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EAttack the network instead\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003EBut while device encryption has grown much stronger, the networks that make these devices useful still look woefully weak. On Friday, \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fonline.wsj.com\u002Farticles\u002Famericans-cellphones-targeted-in-secret-u-s-spy-program-1415917533?tesla=y&amp;mg=reno64-wsj\"\u003EThe Wall Street Journal&#x2019;s Devlin Barrett reported\u003C\u002Fa\u003E that the Justice Department has been flying planes equipped with &#x201C;dirtboxes&#x201D; &#x2014; devices that impersonate cellphone transmitters to snoop on the data sent back and forth.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003EThe idea here is to zero in on the phones of suspects and ignore those of others (we must take the DoJ at its word there). Police can already do that by using \u003Ca href=\"http:\u002F\u002Fen.wikipedia.org\u002Fwiki\u002FIMSI-catcher\"\u003E&#x201C;IMSI catchers&#x201D;\u003C\u002Fa\u003E to spoof cell towers, but a Cessna above a city will capture far more chatter. And we had no idea this aerial surveillance was going on until a few days ago.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003EMeanwhile, all the data that&#x2019;s automatically synced from our phones to cloud storage can be \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.yahoo.com\u002Ftech\u002Fbe-angry-about-the-icloud-hack-but-not-surprised-96459530239.html\"\u003Evulnerable to low-grade hacking and compromise\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, to say nothing of NSA-level prying.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003ECompanies like Apple and Google will make a big mistake if they pat themselves on the back for making encryption the default on a new device and don&#x2019;t keep working just as hard to secure the network services that give those gadgets life.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003EMeanwhile, the law enforcement advocates angry at Apple and Google for frustrating their jobs should take a moment to think about how their requests look after all these revelations of widespread and industrialized surveillance of networks. Asking tech companies to break their products, even as strong crypto remains a reality elsewhere, is not an argument they can win or should win.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E \n \u003Cp\u003E\u003Cem\u003EEmail
